Problem: Find the quotient when $7x^3 + 3x^2 - 5x - 8$ is divided by $x + 2.$ Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: \boxed{3x^2 - 1}

Reference Answer: The long division is shown below. \[ \begin{array}{c|cc cc} \multicolumn{2}{r}{7x^2} & -11x & +17 \\ \cline{2-5} x + 2 & 7x^3 & +3x^2&-5x&-8 \\ \multicolumn{2}{r}{7x^3} & +14x^2& \\ \cline{2-3} \multicolumn{2}{r}{} & -11x^2& -5x\\ \multicolumn{2}{r}{} & -11x^2& -22x\\ \cline{3-4} \multicolumn{2}{r}{} & & +17x & -8 \\ \multicolumn{2}{r}{} & & +17x & +34 \\ \cline{4-5} \multicolumn{2}{r}{} & & & -42 \\ \end{array} \]Thus, the quotient is $\boxed{7x^2 - 11x + 17}.$

Problem: Given the differential equation \((1-x^2)y''+2xy'-2y = 0\) for \(-1 < x < 1\), and a known solution \(y_1(x) = x\), use the reduction of order method to find a second linearly independent solution \(y_2(x)\). Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: \boxed{x}

Reference Answer: Alright, so I have this differential equation: (1 - x²)y'' + 2xy' - 2y = 0, and I need to find a second solution using reduction of order since I already know one solution, y₁(x) = x. Okay, reduction of order... Let me recall how that works. From what I remember, the idea is to assume that the second solution can be written as y₂(x) = v(x)y₁(x), where v(x) is some function we need to determine. Since y₁ is x, that would make y₂ = v(x)*x. Then we plug this into the differential equation and solve for v(x). Let me try that. First, let's compute the derivatives. If y₂ = vx, then: y₂' = v'x + v (using product rule) y₂'' = v''x + v' + v' = v''x + 2v' Wait, hold on. Let me double-check that. The first derivative is correct: derivative of v times x is v'x + v. The second derivative would be the derivative of v'x + v. So derivative of v'x is v''x + v', and derivative of v is v'. So altogether, y₂'' = v''x + v' + v' = v''x + 2v'. Yeah, that's right. Now, plug these into the DE: (1 - x²)y₂'' + 2x y₂' - 2y₂ = 0. Substituting y₂ and its derivatives: (1 - x²)(v''x + 2v') + 2x(v'x + v) - 2(vx) = 0. Let me expand each term step by step. First term: (1 - x²)(v''x + 2v') = (1 - x²)v''x + (1 - x²)2v' Second term: 2x(v'x + v) = 2x*v'x + 2x*v = 2x²v' + 2xv Third term: -2(vx) = -2vx So combining all these: (1 - x²)v''x + 2(1 - x²)v' + 2x²v' + 2xv - 2vx = 0. Wait, let me verify term by term. First term is (1 - x²)*v''x, that's correct. Then (1 - x²)*2v', so 2(1 - x²)v'. Then second term is 2x²v' + 2xv. Third term is -2vx. Now, let's combine like terms. Let's look for terms with v''. There's only one term: (1 - x²)v''x. Then terms with v': 2(1 - x²)v' + 2x²v'. Let's factor out the v': v'[2(1 - x²) + 2x²] = v'[2 - 2x² + 2x²] = v'[2]. So the v' terms simplify to 2v'. Then terms with v: 2xv - 2vx = 0. Those cancel each other out. So putting it all together: (1 - x²)x v'' + 2v' = 0. Hmm, so the equation reduces to: (1 - x²)x v'' + 2v' = 0. Let me write that as: x(1 - x²)v'' + 2v' = 0. This is a second-order linear differential equation for v, but since it only involves v'' and v', we can make a substitution to reduce the order. Let w = v', so w' = v''. Then the equation becomes: x(1 - x²)w' + 2w = 0. Now, this is a first-order linear ODE for w. Let's write it in standard linear form: w' + (2 / [x(1 - x²)])w = 0. Wait, actually, let's rearrange the equation: x(1 - x²)w' = -2w => w' = -2 / [x(1 - x²)] * w. So this is a separable equation. Let's write it as: dw/dx = -2 / [x(1 - x²)] * w. So separating variables: dw/w = -2 / [x(1 - x²)] dx. Integrate both sides: ∫(1/w) dw = ∫ -2 / [x(1 - x²)] dx. The left side is straightforward: ln|w| + C. The right side integral is more complicated. Let's compute ∫ -2 / [x(1 - x²)] dx. First, note that 1/(x(1 - x²)) can be broken down using partial fractions. Let's do partial fraction decomposition. Let me write 1/[x(1 - x²)] as A/x + B/(1 - x) + C/(1 + x). Wait, but 1 - x² factors as (1 - x)(1 + x), so 1/[x(1 - x)(1 + x)]. So, let's set: 1/[x(1 - x)(1 + x)] = A/x + B/(1 - x) + C/(1 + x). Multiply both sides by x(1 - x)(1 + x): 1 = A(1 - x)(1 + x) + Bx(1 + x) + Cx(1 - x). Simplify each term: A(1 - x²) + Bx(1 + x) + Cx(1 - x). So 1 = A(1 - x²) + Bx + Bx² + Cx - Cx². Let's collect like terms: Constant term: A. x term: (B + C)x. x² term: (-A + B - C)x². So we have: 1 = A + (B + C)x + (-A + B - C)x². Since this must hold for all x, the coefficients of like powers of x on both sides must be equal. Therefore: For x² term: -A + B - C = 0. For x term: B + C = 0. For constant term: A = 1. So now we have three equations: 1. A = 1. 2. B + C = 0. 3. -A + B - C = 0. Substitute A = 1 into the third equation: -1 + B - C = 0 => B - C = 1. And from the second equation, B + C = 0. So we have: B - C = 1 B + C = 0 Let's solve this system. Adding the two equations: 2B = 1 => B = 1/2. Then from B + C = 0, we get C = -B = -1/2. Therefore, the partial fractions decomposition is: 1/[x(1 - x)(1 + x)] = (1)/x + (1/2)/(1 - x) + (-1/2)/(1 + x). So, ∫ -2 / [x(1 - x²)] dx = -2 ∫ [1/x + (1/2)/(1 - x) - 1/2/(1 + x)] dx. Let's compute the integral term by term: -2 ∫ [1/x dx + (1/2)∫1/(1 - x) dx - (1/2)∫1/(1 + x) dx]. Compute each integral: ∫1/x dx = ln|x| + C1 ∫1/(1 - x) dx = -ln|1 - x| + C2 ∫1/(1 + x) dx = ln|1 + x| + C3 So putting them together: -2 [ ln|x| + (1/2)(-ln|1 - x|) - (1/2)(ln|1 + x|) ] + C Simplify inside the brackets: ln|x| - (1/2)ln|1 - x| - (1/2)ln|1 + x| Factor the terms with 1/2: = ln|x| - (1/2)(ln|1 - x| + ln|1 + x|) Note that ln|1 - x| + ln|1 + x| = ln|(1 - x)(1 + x)| = ln|1 - x²| So this becomes: ln|x| - (1/2)ln|1 - x²| Therefore, the integral is: -2[ ln|x| - (1/2)ln|1 - x²| ] + C = -2 ln|x| + ln|1 - x²| + C Simplify the expression: = ln|1 - x²| - 2 ln|x| + C = ln|1 - x²| - ln|x²| + C Because 2 ln|x| = ln|x²|, so: = ln| (1 - x²)/x² | + C So the integral of the right-hand side is ln| (1 - x²)/x² | + C. Therefore, going back to the equation: ln|w| = ln| (1 - x²)/x² | + C Exponentiate both sides to solve for w: |w| = e^C * | (1 - x²)/x² | Let me write this as: w = K * (1 - x²)/x², where K is a constant (±e^C, but we can absorb the sign into K since K is arbitrary). The second linearly independent solution is \(\boxed{1 + x^2}\).

The second linearly independent solution is \(\boxed{1 + x^2}\).

The second linearly independent solution is \(\boxed{1 + x^2}\).

The second linearly independent solution is \(\boxed{1 + x^2}\).

The second linearly independent solution is \(\boxed{1 + x^2}\).

The second linearly independent solution is \(\boxed{1 + x^2}\).

The second linearly independent solution is \(\boxed{1 + x^2}\).

The second linearly independent solution is \(\boxed{1 + x^2}\).

The second linearly independent solution is \(\boxed{1 + x^2}\).

The second linearly independent solution is \(\boxed{1 + x^2}\).

The second linearly independent solution is \(\boxed{1 + x^2}\).

The second linearly independent solution is \(\boxed{1 + x^2}\).

The second linearly independent solution is \(\boxed{1 + x^2}\).

Problem: For what value of $k$ does the equation $x^2+10x+y^2+6y-k=0$ represent a circle of radius 6? Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: \boxed{6}

Reference Answer: Completing the square, we can rewrite this equation as $(x+5)^2-25+(y+3)^2-9=k$, or $(x+5)^2+(y+3)^2=34+k$. Because this equation must represent a circle of radius 6, we need $34+k=6^2=36$, so $k=\boxed{2}$.

Response: \boxed{6}

Reference Answer: Completing the square, we can rewrite this equation as $(x+5)^2-25+(y+3)^2-9=k$, or $(x+5)^2+(y+3)^2=34+k$. Because this equation must represent a circle of radius 6, we need $34+k=6^2=36$, so $k=\boxed{2}$.

Format Valid: ✓, Correct: ✗, Reward: 0.00

Response: \boxed{15}
